Since the slope of the line is $\frac{2}{5},$ the line rises 2 units vertically for every 5 horizontal units.  Thus, a possible direction vector is $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = (0,0);
B = (5,0);
C = (5,2);

draw(A--B--C);
draw(A--C,red,Arrow(6));

label("$5$", (A + B)/2, S);
label("$2$", (B + C)/2, E);
[/asy]

This means any nonzero scalar multiple of $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}$ is a possible direction vector.  The possible options are then $\boxed{\text{B, E, G}}.$

The characteristic polynomial of $\mathbf{A}$ is
\[\begin{vmatrix} 15 - t & 25 \\ -9 & -15 - t \end{vmatrix} = (15 - t)(-15 - t) - (-9)(25) = t^2 + 30t.\]Hence, the eigenvalues of $\mathbf{A}$ are 0 and $-30.$

Since $\mathbf{A}$ is not diagonalizable (because it only has one eigenvalue), we cannot write
\[\mathbf{A}^n = c_1 \mathbf{A} + c_2 \mathbf{A}^2 + \dots + c_n \mathbf{A}^n\]for some coefficients $c_1,$ $c_2,$ $\dots,$ $c_n.$

However, we can write $\mathbf{A}^n$ in terms of $\mathbf{I}$ and $\mathbf{A}$ as follows.  Note that $\mathbf{A}^2 = 15 \mathbf{A} + 25 \mathbf{I}$ and
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{A}^3 &= \mathbf{A} (\mathbf{A}^2) \\
&= \mathbf{A} (15 \mathbf{A} + 25 \mathbf{I}) \\
&= 15 \mathbf{A}^2 + 25 \mathbf{A} \\
&= 15 (15 \mathbf{A} + 25 \mathbf{I}) + 25 \mathbf{A} \\
&= 250 \mathbf{I} + 260 \mathbf{A}.
\end{align*}Then
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{I} + 2 \mathbf{A} + 3 \mathbf{A}^2 + 4 \mathbf{A}^3 + \dotsb &= \mathbf{I} + 2 \mathbf{A} + 3 (15 \mathbf{A} + 25 \mathbf{I}) + 4 (250 \mathbf{I} + 260 \mathbf{A}) + \dotsb \\
&= \mathbf{I} + 2 \mathbf{A} + 45 \mathbf{A} + 1000 \mathbf{I} + 1040 \mathbf{A} + \dotsb \\
&= \boxed{1000 \mathbf{I} + 1082 \mathbf{A}} + \dotsb.
\end{align*}

The line containing $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ can be parameterized by
\[\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{a} + t (\mathbf{b} - \mathbf{a}) = \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]Since $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ must be equal to the angle between $\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{c}.$  Thus,

Let $\mathbf{c} = \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix}.$

Since $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ and $\mathbf{c}$ are collinear, we can write
\[\mathbf{c} = k \mathbf{a} + (1 - k) \mathbf{b}\]for some scalar $k.$

Also, we want $\mathbf{b}$ to bisect the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ so we want $\mathbf{a} - \mathbf{b}$ to be orthogonal to $\mathbf{c} - \mathbf{b}.$  This gives us the equation
\[(\mathbf{a} - \mathbf{b}) \cdot (\mathbf{c} - \mathbf{b}) = 0.\]Expanding, we get
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{a} - \mathbf{b}) \cdot (\mathbf{c} - \mathbf{b}) &= \begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} \cdot (\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}) \\
&= (-3)(x - 1) + 2(y - 3) = 0.
\end{align*}Hence, $3x - 2y = -5.$

Since $\mathbf{c}$ is collinear with $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b},$
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = k \begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} + (1 - k) \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}.\]Thus, $x = -2k + k + 1 - k,$ $y = 5k + 3k - 3k.$

Hence, $3x - 2y = -5$ becomes $3(-2k + k + 1 - k) - 2(5k - 3k) = -5,$ or $-k = -\frac{1}{2}.$  Then
\[\mathbf{c} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} \frac{3}{2} \\ \frac{15}{2} \end{pmatrix}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Let the circumcenter $O$ of triangle $ABC$ be the origin.  Then
\[\overrightarrow{G} = \frac{\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}}{3}\]and $\overrightarrow{H} = \overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C},$ so
\[\overrightarrow{F} = \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}).\]Then
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= \|\overrightarrow{A} - \overrightarrow{F}\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}) \right\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \frac{1}{3} \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{B} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{C} \right\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \|\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \cdot (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{A} + 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 4 \overrightarrow{C} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}  - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}), \\
CF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Thus, $AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 = \boxed{3R^2}.$

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/precalculus/1133_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
We make use of the fact that $G$ and $H$ are isogonal conjugates, which implies that $H$ lies on $\odot(ABC).$  Furthermore, it is well known that $GH = \frac{2}{3} OH,$ so $F$ lies on $\odot(OGH)$ with center $O.$

[asy]
unitsize(2 cm);

pair A, B, C, F, G, H, O;

A = (0,1.5);
B = (-0.9,0);
C = (1,0);
O = (0,0);
G = (A + B + C)/3;
H = extension(B,bisectorpoint(C,B,A),C,bisectorpoint(B,C,A));

F = (G + H)/2;

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(circumcircle(A,B,C));
draw(G--H,dashed);
draw(O--G);
draw(O--H);
draw(O--F);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$G$", G, N);
label("$H$", H, S);
label("$O$", O, S);
label("$F$", F, E);
[/asy]

Let $R$ be the circumradius of $\triangle ABC.$  By power of a point from $O,$
\[OG \cdot OH = R^2 - OG^2 = R^2 - \frac{1}{9} \left( OA^2 + OB^2 + OC^2 \right).\]First of all,
\begin{align*}
OA^2 &= R^2 - (R - d_A)^2 \\
&= 2Rd_A - d_A^2.
\end{align*}Also, by Stewart's Theorem on triangle $ABC,$
\[d_A d_B c + d_BC_A a + c_A b d_A = 2d_A^2 m_A + 2bm_A^2 + 2cm_A^2,\]so
\[d_A^2 = \frac{2m_B^2 + 2m_C^2 - a^2}{4}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
OA^2 &= 2Rd_A - d_A^2 \\
&= 2R \cdot \frac{\sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2}}{2} - \frac{2m_B^2 + 2m_C^2 - a^2}{4} \\
&= R \sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2} - \frac{m_B^2 + m_C^2}{2} + \frac{a^2}{4}.
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
OB^2 &= R \sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} - \frac{m_C^2 + m_A^2}{2} + \frac{b^2}{4}, \\
OC^2 &= R \sqrt{2a^2 + 2b^2 - c^2} - \frac{m_A^2 + m_B^2}{2} + \frac{c^2}{4}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\begin{align*}
R^2 - \frac{1}{9} \left( OA^2 + OB^2 + OC^2 \right) &= R^2 - \frac{1}{9} \left[ \left( R \sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2} - \frac{m_B^2 + m_C^2}{2} + \frac{a^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} - \frac{m_C^2 + m_A^2}{2} + \frac{b^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2a^2 + 2b^2 - c^2} - \frac{m_A^2 + m_B^2}{2} + \frac{c^2}{4} \right) \right] \\
&= \frac{2R^2}{9} + R \cdot \frac{1}{9} (a^2 + b^2 + c^2) \\
&\quad - \frac{1}{18} (m_A^2 + m_B^2 + m_C^2) \\
&\quad + \frac{1}{18} \left( \sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2}^2 + \sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2}^2 + \sqrt{2a^2 + 2b^2 - c^2}^2 \right).
\end{align*}Now,
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= AO^2 - OF^2 \\
&= R^2 - \frac{1}{4} OG^2 \\
&= R^2 - \frac{1}{4} \cdot \frac{1}{9} \left( OA^2 + OB^2 + OC^2 \right) \\
&= R^2 - \frac{1}{36} \left[ \left( R \sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2} - \frac{m_B^2 + m_C^2}{2} + \frac{a^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} - \frac{m_C^2 + m_A^2}{2} + \frac{b^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2a^2 + 2b^2 - c^2} - \frac{m_A^2 + m_B^2}{2} + \frac{c^2}{4} \right) \right].
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= R^2 - \frac{1}{36} \left[ \left( R \sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} - \frac{m_C^2 + m_A^2}{2} + \frac{b^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2a^2 + 2b^2 - c^2} - \frac{m_A^2 + m_B^2}{2} + \frac{c^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2} - \frac{m_B^2 + m_C^2}{2} + \frac{a^2}{4} \right) \right], \\
CF^2 &= R^2 - \frac{1}{36} \left[ \left( R \sqrt{2a^2 + 2b^2 - c^2} - \frac{m_A^2 + m_B^2}{2} + \frac{c^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2} - \frac{m_B^2 + m_C^2}{2} + \frac{a^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} - \frac{m_C^2 + m_A^2}{2} + \frac{b^2}{4} \right) \right].
\end{align*}Hence,
\begin{align*}
AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 &= 3R^2 - \frac{1}{36} \cdot 3 \left[ \left( R \sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2} - \frac{m_B^2 + m_C^2}{2} + \frac{a^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} - \frac{m_C^2 + m_A^2}{2} + \frac{b^2}{4} \right) \right. \\
&\quad \left. + \left( R \sqrt{2a^2 + 2b^2 - c^2} - \frac{m_A^2 + m_B^2}{2} + \frac{c^2}{4} \right) \right] \\
&= 3R^2 - \frac{1}{4} (3R^2 - 4Rr - r^2) \\
&\quad + \frac{1}{36} \left[ \left( \sqrt{2b^2 + 2c^2 - a^2} + \sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} + \sqrt{2a^2 + 2b^2 - c^2} \right)^2 \right] \\
&= \boxed{4Rr + r^2 + \frac{1}{9} (a^2 + b^2 + c^2)}.
\end{align*}

Extracting Answer from: 
To project onto the $y$-axis, we want to keep the $y$-coordinate of each vector, and set the $x$-coordinate to zero. This can be accomplished with the following matrix:
$$ \begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} $$ 

To see why this works, consider the following vector:
$$\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} $$ 
When we multiply this vector by the projection matrix, we get:
$$ \begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \cdot x + 0 \cdot y \\ 0 \cdot x + 1 \cdot y \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ y \end{pmatrix} $$ 
As expected, the $y$-coordinate is unchanged while the $x$-coordinate is set to zero, resulting in a vector that lies on the $y$-axis.

Extracting Answer from: 
From the formula for a projection,
\[\operatorname{proj}_{\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\|^2} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]This vector has magnitude
\[\left\| \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \cdot 2 = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2}.\]Thus, we want $\frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2} = \sqrt{3}.$  Equivalently, $|a \sqrt{3} + b| = 2 \sqrt{3},$ or $(a \sqrt{3} + b)^2 = 12.$

Also, $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ so
\[(2 \sqrt{3} + 4b)^2 = 12.\]Then $2 \sqrt{3} + 4b = \pm 2 \sqrt{3}.$  This leads to the solutions $b = -\sqrt{3}$ and $b = 0,$ which in turn leads to the values $a = \boxed{-1}$ and $a = \boxed{2}.$

Since $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix}$ is projected onto $\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$, the resulting vector is of the form $k\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ for some scalar $k$. Let $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix}.$  Then from the equation $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 2,$ $x + y + z = 2.$

\[\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -y + z \\ x - z \\ -x + y \end{pmatrix}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
-y + z &= 1, \\
x - z &= -2, \\
-x + y &= 1.
\end{align*}Solving this system, along with the equation $x + y + z = 2,$ we find $x = -\frac{1}{3},$ $y = \frac{2}{3},$ and $z = \frac{5}{3}.$  Thus, $\mathbf{v} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -1/3 \\ 2/3 \\ 5/3 \end{pmatrix}}.$

\[\frac{1}{\sqrt{13}} \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} \frac{2}{\sqrt{13}} \\ -\frac{3}{\sqrt{13}} \end{pmatrix}\]to get
\[\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \frac{2}{\sqrt{13}} \\ -\frac{3}{\sqrt{13}} \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\frac{6}{\sqrt{13}}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
From product-to-sum,
\[\sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ = \frac{1}{2} [\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ].\]Thus, we can make the sum telescope:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ &= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \frac{\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2 \sin 5^\circ} [(\cos 0^\circ - \cos 10^\circ) + (\cos 5^\circ - \cos 15^\circ) + (\cos 10^\circ - \cos 20^\circ) + \\
&\quad + \dots + (\cos 165^\circ - \cos 175^\circ) + (\cos 170^\circ - \cos 180^\circ)] \\
&= \frac{\cos 0^\circ + \cos 5^\circ - \cos 175^\circ - \cos 180^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 + 2 \cos 5^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ}.
\end{align*}Then by the double-angle formulas,
\begin{align*}
\frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} &= \frac{1 + 2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ - 1}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 2.5^\circ}{\sin 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \cot 2.5^\circ \\
&= \tan 87.5^\circ.
\end{align*}Thus, $r = \boxed{87.5}.$

We remember that $\sin 5x^\circ$ is a degree-$4$ polynomial in $\sin x^\circ$; to be specific,
\[\sin 5x^\circ = 16 \sin^5 x^\circ - 20 \sin^3 x^\circ + 5 \sin x^\circ.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ &= \sum_{k=1}^{35} \left( 16 \sin^5 k^\circ - 20 \sin^3 k^\circ + 5 \sin k^\circ \right) \\
&= \sum_{k=1}^{35} 4 \sin k^\circ (4-6\sin^2 k^\circ + 3 \sin^4 k^\circ) \\
&= 4\sin 1^\circ (4-6\sin^2 1^\circ + 3\sin^4 1^\circ) + 4\sin 2^\circ (4-6\sin^2 2^\circ + 3\sin^4 2^\circ) + \dots + 4\sin 35^\circ (4-6\sin^2 35^\circ + 3\sin^4 35^\circ).
\end{align*}For each $k,$ $0 < \sin k^\circ < 1,$ so the quantity $4-6\sin^2 k^\circ + 3\sin^4 k^\circ$ is certainly positive. Thus, we can apply the tangent double-angle formula
\[\tan 2x^\circ = \frac{2 \tan x^\circ}{1-\tan^2 x^\circ}\]to each $\sin k^\circ$ term all simultaneously. With algebra, we can show that this means that there is some step $k^\circ$ of $1^\circ$ such that the sum telescopes:
\[\sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ = \tan r^\circ,\]where $r$ is one such step at which the series telescopes. (Note that such a $r$ need not be unique.)

For a given $r,$ we have the following telescoping sum identity:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=1}^{35} \frac{\sin 10k^\circ}{\cos 10k^\circ} &= \sum_{k=1}^{35} \tan 10k^\circ - \tan 10(k-1)^\circ \\
&= \tan 350^\circ - \tan 10^\circ \\
&= \tan 10^\circ.
\end{align*}In particular, if we plug in $5k = 10 (2k),$ then
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=1}^{35} \tan 10k^\circ &= 2 \sum_{k=1}^{35} \tan 5k^\circ \cdot \frac{1}{\cos 10k^\circ} \\
&= 2 \sum_{k=1}^{35} \tan 5k^\circ \cdot \frac{1}{\cos 10(k-18)^\circ}.
\end{align*}Let $T = \tan r^\circ/\cos 10^\circ,$ so that $\tan r^\circ = T \cos 10^\circ,$ and hence
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=1}^{35} \tan 10k^\circ &= 2T\sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin 5k^\circ \cos 10(17-k)^\circ \\
&= 2T\sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin 5k^\circ (\cos 10k^\circ \cos 170^\circ + \sin 10k^\circ \sin 170^\circ) \\
&= 2T\sum_{k=1}^{35} \left( \sin 5k^\circ \cos 10k^\circ \cos 170^\circ + \frac{\sin 5k^\circ}{2} \right) \\
&= 2T\sum_{k=1}^{35} \left( \frac{1}{2} \sin 20k^\circ \cos 170^\circ + \frac{\sin 5k^\circ}{2} \right) \\
&= T \sum_{k=1}^{35} \cos 20k^\circ + \sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin 5k^\circ.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[T = \frac{\sum_{k=1}^{35} \sin 5k^\circ}{\sum_{k=1}^{35} \cos 20k^\circ}.\]Thus, $r$ is a multiple of $\boxed{10^\circ}.$

&= 2 + 2 \cdot \frac{\cos^2 \theta}{\sin^2 \theta} \\

\end{align*}

Let $\mathbf{a}$ be an arbitrary vector.  Let $\mathbf{p}$ be the projection of $\mathbf{a}$ onto $\mathbf{v},$ so $\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a},$ and let $\mathbf{r}$ be the reflection of $\mathbf{a}$ over $\mathbf{v},$ to $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{R} \mathbf{a}.$

Note that $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r}.$  We can use this to find the relationship between $\mathbf{R}$ and $\mathbf{P}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair D, P, R, V;

D = (3,2);
V = (1.5,2);
R = reflect((0,0),D)*(V);
P = (V + R)/2;

draw((-1,0)--(4,0));
draw((0,-1)--(0,3));
draw((0,0)--D,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--V,red,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--R,blue,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--P,green,Arrow(6));
draw(V--R,dashed);

label("$\mathbf{v}$", D, NE);
label("$\mathbf{p}$", P, S);
label("$\mathbf{a}$", V, N);
label("$\mathbf{r}$", R, SE);
[/asy]

Since $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r},$ $\mathbf{p} = \frac{\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{r}}{2},$ so
\[\mathbf{r} = 2 \mathbf{p} - \mathbf{a}.\]In other words,
\[\mathbf{R} \mathbf{a} = 2 \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a} - \mathbf{I} \mathbf{a}.\]Since this holds for all vectors $\mathbf{a},$
\[\mathbf{R} = 2 \mathbf{P} - \mathbf{I}.\]Thus, $(a,b) = \boxed{(2,-1)}.$

Since $\mathbf{P}$ is a projection matrix onto a vector $\mathbf{v},$
\[\mathbf{P}^2 = \mathbf{P}.\]Also,
\[\mathbf{R}^2 = (\mathbf{P} - \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{v}^\perp})^2 = \mathbf{P}^2 - 2 \mathbf{P} \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{v}^\perp} + \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{v}^\perp}^2.\]Since $\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{v}^\perp}$ is the projection matrix onto a subspace $\mathbf{v}^\perp$ which is orthogonal to $\mathbf{v},$ we have $\mathbf{P} \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{v}^\perp} = \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{v}^\perp} \mathbf{P} = \mathbf{0}.$  Hence,
\[\mathbf{R}^2 = \mathbf{P} - \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{v}^\perp} = \mathbf{P} + \mathbf{R}.\]Hence,
\[\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{R}^2 = (a - 1) \mathbf{P} + b \mathbf{I}.\]Since $\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{R}^2$ is a reflection matrix,
\[\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{R}^2 = \mathbf{R} (\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I}),\]so
\[\mathbf{R} (\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I}) = (a - 1) \mathbf{P} + b \mathbf{I}.\]Let $\mathbf{v}$ be a unit vector.  Then
\[\mathbf{R} (\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I}) \mathbf{v} = (a - 1) \mathbf{P} \mathbf{v} + b \mathbf{v},\]so
\[\mathbf{R} (\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I}) \mathbf{v} = b \mathbf{v}.\]Thus, $\mathbf{v}$ is an eigenvector of both $\mathbf{R} (\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I})$ and $\mathbf{P}.$  We know that the eigenvalues of $\mathbf{P}$ are 0 and 1, so $\mathbf{v}$ is an eigenvector of $\mathbf{P}$ with eigenvalue 1 (since $\mathbf{v}$ is not projected to the origin).  Note that $\mathbf{v}$ is also an eigenvector of the reflection $\mathbf{R}$ with eigenvalue 1.

We claim that $\mathbf{v}$ is also an eigenvector of $\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I}$ with eigenvalue 2.  To see this, note that
\[\|\mathbf{R} \mathbf{v} - \mathbf{v}\| = \|(\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I}) \mathbf{v}\| = 2 \|\mathbf{v}\| = 2.\]Thus, $\mathbf{R} \mathbf{v} - \mathbf{v}$ is a scalar multiple of $\mathbf{v}$ with eigenvalue 2.

Thus, for any unit vector $\mathbf{v}$, there exists a real number $a$ such that
\[(\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I}) \mathbf{v} = 2a \mathbf{v}.\]Taking this equation for a specific unit vector $\mathbf{v}$ and multiplying both sides by $\mathbf{v}^T$, we get
\[(\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I}) \mathbf{v} = 2a.\]Since $a$ can vary depending on $\mathbf{v}$, the coefficients $a$ will vanish from the left hand side of the equation. Thus,
\[\mathbf{R} - \mathbf{I} = 2a \mathbf{P} + b \mathbf{I}\]for some real numbers $a$ and $b$.  From this equation, we can solve to get
\[\mathbf{R} = (2a + 1) \mathbf{P} + b \mathbf{I}.\]Setting $\mathbf{v}$ to be the standard unit vector $\mathbf{\hat e}_1$, we get that
\[(2a + 1) \begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 \end{pmatrix} + b \begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & -1 \end{pmatrix}.\]Equating entries, we get $2a + b = 1$ and $b = -1$.  Solving gives $(a,b) = \boxed{\left( -\frac{1}{2}, -1 \right)}$.

Option (B) is not a valid parameterization, as $t$ ranges through all the reals, the expression does not necessarily trace out all points in the line that passes through $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$.

Option (F) is not a valid parameterization, as $t$ ranges through all the reals, the expression does not necessarily trace out all points in the line that passes through $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}.$

To convert from polar coordinates to rectangular coordinates, we use the formulas $x = r \cos \theta$ and $y = r \sin \theta$. In this case, $r = 10$ and $\theta = \frac{\pi}{4}$, so we have:

\begin{align*}
x &= r \cos \theta = 10 \cos \frac{\pi}{4} = 10 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} = 5 \sqrt{2} \\
y &= r \sin \theta = 10 \sin \frac{\pi}{4} = 10 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} = 5 \sqrt{2}
\end{align*}

Therefore, the rectangular coordinates of the point $\left(10, \frac{\pi}{4}\right)$ in polar coordinates are $\left(5\sqrt{2},5\sqrt{2}\right)$.

Extracting Answer from: 
We can compute the angle between two vectors $\mathbf{v}$ and $\mathbf{w}$ using the dot product, as
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}}{\|\mathbf{v}\| \|\mathbf{w}\|}.\]In this case, the direction vectors of the lines are $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ and $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix},$ so we can compute the angle between them as follows:
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| \left\| \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} = \frac{1}{3}.\]Note that if the lines form an acute angle at $P,$ then they must intersect, so $P$ exists.

[asy]
import three;

size(200);
currentprojection = perspective(6,3,2);

triple I = (1,0,0), J = (0,1,0), K = (0,0,1);
triple O = (0,-0.5,0), P = (7/3,-1/3,-7/3);
triple A = (-1,-3,-1), B = (-3,2,-10);
triple Q = extension(A, A + 2*I + J, B, B + I - J);

draw(O--2*I, Arrow3(6));
draw(O--2*J, Arrow3(6));
draw(O--2*K, Arrow3(6));
draw(O--A,red,Arrow3(6));
draw(O--B,red,Arrow3(6));
draw(O--Q,red,Arrow3(6));
draw(P--Q,dashed);

label("$\mathbf{i}$", 2*I, N);
label("$\mathbf{j}$", 2*J, E);
label("$\mathbf{k}$", 2*K, N);
label("$L_1$", A, S);
label("$L_2$", B, E);
label("$P$", P, W);
[/asy]

The shortest distance between two skew lines is the distance between the two lines.  Let $Q$ be the foot of the perpendicular from $\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ 2 \\ -10 \end{pmatrix}$ to the line through $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -3 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix}$ parallel to $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.$  Then
\[\overrightarrow{PQ} = \begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ 2 \\ -10 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -3 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix} + s \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} =\begin{pmatrix} -2 + 2s \\ 5 + s \\ -9 + s \end{pmatrix}.\]This vector is orthogonal to $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix},$ so
\[\begin{pmatrix} -2 + 2s \\ 5 + s \\ -9 + s \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} = 0.\]Then $2s - 2 = -s - 5 + 4s - 18 = 0.$  Solving, we find $s = -\frac{7}{3}.$  Hence,
\[\overrightarrow{PQ} = \begin{pmatrix} \frac{4}{3} \\ \frac{4}{3} \\ -\frac{8}{3} \end{pmatrix}.\]Finally, $PQ = \|\overrightarrow{PQ}\| = 4,$ so the acute angle between the lines is $\boxed{70.53^\circ}.$

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/precalculus/254_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
A point on the plane is given by
\[\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 5 \\ 6 \end{pmatrix}\]for some scalar $t,$ and a point on the line is given by
\[\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ 0 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} + s \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}\]for some scalar $s.$  Thus, we want
\[\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 5 \\ 6 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ 0 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} + s \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.\]Equating entries, we get
\begin{align*}
1 + 4t &= -1 - s, \\
2 + 5t &= -s, \\
3 + 6t &= 5 + 2s.
\end{align*}From the first equation, $s = -4t - 2.$  Substituting into the second equation, we get $t = -\frac{2}{3}.$  Then $s = \frac{2}{3}.$  Substituting into the third equation, we get
\[-\frac{4}{3} = 5 - \frac{4}{3},\]which is not true.  Therefore, the line and the plane do not intersect.

[asy]
import three;

Let $x = \cos 2t$ and $y = \cos^2 t$.  Then
\[y = \cos^2 t = \frac{\cos 2t + 1}{2} = \frac{x + 1}{2}.\]Furthermore, $x = \cos 2t$ varies between $-1$ and 1, so the endpoints of the line segments are $(-1,0)$ and $(1,1).$  Hence, the length of the line segment is $\sqrt{2^2 + 1^2} = \boxed{\sqrt{5}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
From the equation, $\cos 2t = 1 - 2 \sin^2 t.$  Let $y = \cos^2 t.$  Then $2y - 1 = 2 \sin^2 t,$ so $\cos 2t = 1 - 2y.$

Hence,
\begin{align*}
x &= 1 - 2y, \\
y &= y.
\end{align*}We can then compute $dx$ and $dy,$ then take the square root of $dx^2 + dy^2.$  We get
\begin{align*}
dx &= -2 \, dy, \\
dy &= -\sin 2t \, dt = -2 \sin t \cos t \, dt,
\end{align*}so
\[\sqrt{dx^2 + dy^2} = \sqrt{4 \cos^2 t \sin^2 t + 4 \sin^4 t} \, dt = 2 \sin t \sqrt{\cos^2 t + \sin^2 t} \, dt = 2 \sin t \, dt.\]The parameterization $(x,y) = (\cos 2t, \cos^2 t)$ traces out the segment connecting $(1,1)$ to $(-1,1).$  Thus,
\[\int_0^{\pi/4} 2 \sin t \, dt = 2 - \sqrt{2}.\]Then we reflect the result across the $y$-axis, so the length of the line segment is $2\left(2 - \sqrt{2}\right) = \boxed{4 - 2 \sqrt{2}}.$

Applying the triple angle formula, we get
\begin{align*}
\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x &= \left( \frac{3}{4} \sin 2x - \frac{1}{4} \sin 6x \right) \cos 6x + \left( \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x + \frac{1}{4} \cos 6x \right) \sin 6x \\
&= \frac{3}{4} \sin 2x \cos 6x + \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x \sin 6x.
\end{align*}Then by the angle addition formula,
\[\frac{3}{4} \sin 2x \cos 6x + \frac{3}{4} \cos 2x \sin 6x = \frac{3}{4} \sin (2x + 6x) = \frac{3}{4} \sin 8x.\]Thus, $a + b = \frac{3}{4} + 8 = \boxed{\frac{35}{4}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We can write
\begin{align*}
\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x &= (\sin^2 2x)(\sin 2x \cos 6x) + (\cos^2 2x)(\cos 2x \sin 6x) \\
&= (\sin^2 2x)(\sin 2x \cos 6x) + (\cos^2 2x)(\sin 6x \cos 2x) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin^2 4x \cdot 2 \sin 2x \cos 6x + \frac{1}{2} \cos^2 4x \cdot 2 \sin 6x \cos 2x \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin^2 4x \cdot \sin 8x + \frac{1}{2} \cos^2 4x \cdot \sin 8x.
\end{align*}Now,
\[\frac{1}{2} \sin^2 4x + \frac{1}{2} \cos^2 4x = \frac{1}{2},\]so by the angle addition formula,
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{2} \sin^2 4x \cdot \sin 8x + \frac{1}{2} \cos^2 4x \cdot \sin 8x &= \frac{1}{2} \sin 8x \cdot \left[ \frac{1}{2} \sin^2 4x + \frac{1}{2} \cos^2 4x \right] \\
&= \frac{1}{4} \sin 8x.
\end{align*}Thus, $a + b = \boxed{\frac{1}{4}}.$

Then $BD = \frac{x}{3/2} = \frac{2x}{3},$ and $CD = \frac{x}{1/2} = 2x,$ so
\[BC = BD + DC = \frac{2x}{3} + 2x = \frac{8x}{3}.\]Since $BC = 32,$ $x = 12.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We have that $\tan B = \frac{3}{2},$ so $\cos B = \frac{2}{\sqrt{13}}$ and $\sin B = \frac{3}{\sqrt{13}}.$

We have that $\tan C = \frac{1}{2},$ so $\cos C = \frac{2 \sqrt{5}}{5}$ and $\sin C = \frac{\sqrt{5}}{5}.$

Therefore,
\begin{align*}
AC &= \frac{BC}{\cos C} = \frac{32}{(2 \sqrt{5}/5)} = \frac{80}{\sqrt{5}}, \\
AB &= \frac{BC}{\cos B} = \frac{32}{(2/\sqrt{13})} = 16 \sqrt{13}, \\
\end{align*}and
\begin{align*}
[\triangle ABC] &= \frac{1}{2} AC \cdot AB \sin C \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{80}{\sqrt{5}} \cdot 16 \sqrt{13} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{5}}{5} \\
&= \boxed{256}.
\end{align*}

Since the cosine function has period $360^\circ,$
\[\cos 568^\circ = \cos (568^\circ - 2 \cdot 360^\circ) = \cos (-152^\circ).\]And since the cosine function is even, $\cos (-152^\circ) = \cos 152^\circ,$ so $n = \boxed{152}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Since the cosine function repeats every $360^\circ,$
\[\cos n^\circ = \cos (568^\circ - 360^\circ) = \cos 208^\circ.\]Setting this equal to $\cos n^\circ,$ we have
\[\cos n^\circ = \cos 208^\circ.\]The solutions of $\cos n^\circ = \cos 208^\circ$ are of the form $n = 360^\circ k \pm 208^\circ,$ so
\[n = \pm 208^\circ,\]which gives us $2$ solutions in the interval $[0^\circ,180^\circ]:$

\[n = 152^\circ, \quad n = 208^\circ.\]Thus, the integer $n$ we seek is $\boxed{152}.$

\[\cos A = \frac{10^2 + 10^2 - 12^2}{2 \cdot 10 \cdot 10} = \frac{7}{25}.\]Let $x = AD = DE = CE.$

This problem calls for mass points.  Assign point $A$ a mass of $3$.  Since $AB = AC$ and $D$ is between $A$ and $B$, assign point $D$ a mass of $2$.  Then $B$ has a mass of $1$.  This implies that $BD/DA = 2$.

[asy]

\begin{align*}
10^2 \cdot \frac{14}{2} + x^2 \cdot 14 &= 12^2 \cdot 10 + x^2\cdot12 \\
x^2 &= \frac{600}{7}.
\end{align*}Thus $AD = DE = \boxed{\sqrt{\frac{600}{7}}}$.

\[\tan 2 \theta = \frac{2 \tan \theta}{1 - \tan^2 \theta} = \frac{2 \cdot 2}{1 - 2^2} = -\frac{8}{3}.\]Then

\end{align*}

From the angle addition formula, the numerator is
\begin{align*}
&(\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) + (\cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ -  \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos (5^\circ + 20^\circ) + \cos (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ.
\end{align*}From the sum-to-product formula, $\cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ = 2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ.$

Similarly, the denominator is
\begin{align*}
&\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= (\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ +  \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) - (\sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin (5^\circ + 20^\circ) - \sin (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin 25^\circ - \sin 85^\circ \\
&= -2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ,
\end{align*}so the expression is equal to
\[\frac{2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ}{-2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ} = -\frac{\cos 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ} = -\sqrt{3} = \tan 120^\circ.\]Hence, the smallest such $\theta$ is $\boxed{120^\circ}.$

From the given expression,
\begin{align*}
\tan \theta &= \frac{\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ} \\
&= \frac{(\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) + (\cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ)}{(\cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ) - (\cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ)} \\
&= \frac{\cos 15^\circ + \cos 85^\circ}{\sin 15^\circ - \sin 85^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 15^\circ + \cos 85^\circ}{-2 \sin (-35^\circ) \cos 50^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 15^\circ + \cos 85^\circ}{\sin 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos 50^\circ \cos 35^\circ}{\sin 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 40^\circ}{\sin 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin (55^\circ - 15^\circ)}{\sin 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 55^\circ \cos 15^\circ - \sin 15^\circ \cos 55^\circ}{\sin 55^\circ} \\
&= \cos 15^\circ - \frac{\sin 15^\circ \cos 55^\circ}{\sin 55^\circ} \\
&= \cos 15^\circ - \frac{\sin 15^\circ \cdot \sin 35^\circ}{\sin 55^\circ \cdot \sin 35^\circ} \\
&= \cos 15^\circ - \frac{\sin 15^\circ}{2 \cos 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos^2 15^\circ - \sin 15^\circ \cos 55^\circ}{2 \cos 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 30^\circ + \cos 40^\circ}{\cos 55^\circ}.
\end{align*}Then
\begin{align*}
\tan \theta &= \frac{\cos 30^\circ + \cos 40^\circ}{\cos 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} + \cos 40^\circ}{\cos 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} \cos (45^\circ - 5^\circ)}{\cos 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} (\cos 45^\circ \cos 5^\circ + \sin 45^\circ \sin 5^\circ)}{\cos 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2 \sqrt{2}}}{\cos 55^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 + \sqrt{6}}{4 \cos 55^\circ}.
\end{align*}Therefore, the degree measure of the least positive angle $\theta$ is $\boxed{55^\circ}.$

We can write $\tan x = \sin x$ as $\frac{\sin x}{\cos x} = \sin x,$ so
\[\sin x = \cos x \sin x.\]Then $\sin x - \cos x \sin x = 0,$ or $\sin x (1 - \cos x) = 0.$  Thus, $\sin x = 0$ or $\cos x = 1.$

The solutions to $\sin x = 0$ are $x = 0,$ $\pi,$ and $2 \pi.$

The solution to $\cos x = 1$ is $x = 0.$

Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{0, \pi, 2 \pi}.$

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/precalculus/551_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Since $z^{13} = 1,$ $z^{13} - 1 = 0,$ which factors as
\[(z - 1)(z^{12} + z^{11} + z^{10} + \dots + z^2 + z + 1) = 0.\]Furthermore, $z^{13} - 1 = 0$ tells us that the possible values of $z$ are the 13th roots of unity.

Suppose $z = e^{2 \pi i/13}.$  Then $z^{13} = 1,$ and furthermore,
\[z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}\]consists of the terms $z^k,$ where $k$ is an integer in the set $\{1,3,4,9,10,12\}.$  We can regroup the terms as follows:
\begin{align*}
z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} &= (z^3 + z^{10}) + (z^4 + z^9) + z + (z^{12} + z) \\
&= z^3 (1 + z^7) + z^4 (1 + z^5) + z (1 + z^{11}) + z (1 + z^6) \\
&= z^3 (1 + z^{-6}) + z^4 (1 + z^{-8}) + z (1 + z^2) + z (1 + z^5) \\
&= z^3 (1 + z^7 + z^{12} + z^6) + z^4 (1 + z^5 + z^8 + z^{10}) + z (1 + z^{11} + z^2 + z^3).
\end{align*}By summing geometric series, we get
\begin{align*}
&z^3 (1 + z^7 + z^{12} + z^6) \\
&\quad= z^3 \cdot \frac{z^{4 \cdot 3} - 1}{z^3 - 1} \\
&\quad= z^{15} - z^3 \\
&\quad= z^2 - z^3, \\
&z^4 (1 + z^5 + z^8 + z^{10}) \\
&\quad= z^4 \cdot \frac{z^{4 \cdot 4} - 1}{z^4 - 1} \\
&\quad= z^{16} + z^{12} + z^8 + z^4 \\
&\quad= z^3 + z^2 + z + 1, \\
&z (1 + z^{11} + z^2 + z^3) \\
&\quad= z \cdot \frac{z^{4 \cdot 3} - 1}{z^3 - 1} \\
&\quad= z^{13} - z \\
&\quad= 1 - z.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\[z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} = (z^2 - z^3) + (z^3 + z^2 + z + 1) + (1 - z) = z^2 + 2,\]so the possible values of $z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}$ are $e^{2 \pi i m/13} + 2,$ where $m$ is an integer in the set $\{0,1,2,\dots,12\}.$

In general, if $n$ is relatively prime to 13, then
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k = 1}^{12} e^{2 \pi i k m/13} &= \frac{e^{2 \pi i m/13} - 1}{e^{2 \pi i m/13} - e^{2 \pi i 13m/13}} \cdot \sum_{k = 0}^{12} e^{2 \pi i km/13} \\
&= \frac{e^{2 \pi i m/13} - 1}{e^{2 \pi i m/13} - 1} \cdot 0 \\
&= 0.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}

Since $w^{11}=z$, we also have $z=r^{11}(\cos{\frac{143m\pi}{n}}+i\sin{\frac{143m\pi}{n}})$.
Equating the imaginary parts of the last two expressions and simplifying, we have $\tan{\frac{m\pi}{n}}=\tan{\frac{13m\pi}{n}}\tan{\frac{143m\pi}{n}}$. Note that $n$ must be odd since if $n$ were even, then both $m$ and $2m$ would be marked, implying $m$ and $n$ are not relatively prime.
First assume $\gcd(m,143)=1$. This is valid since $\gcd(m,n)=1$. Then $\tan{\frac{m\pi}{n}}=\tan{\frac{13m\pi}{n}}\tan{\frac{143m\pi}{n}}$ becomes $\frac{\sin{\frac{m\pi}{n}}}{\cos{\frac{m\pi}{n}}}=\frac{\sin{\frac{13m\pi}{n}}}{\cos{\frac{13m\pi}{n}}}\cdot\frac{\sin{\frac{37m\pi}{n}}}{\cos{\frac{37m\pi}{n}}}\cdot\frac{\sin{\frac{67m\pi}{n}}}{\cos{\frac{67m\pi}{n}}}\cdot\frac{\sin{\frac{109m\pi}{n}}}{\cos{\frac{109m\pi}{n}}}$, which becomes $\tan{\frac{m\pi}{n}}=\tan{\frac{\pi}{2}-\frac{13m\pi}{n}}\tan{\frac{\pi}{2}-\frac{37m\pi}{n}}\tan{\frac{\pi}{2}-\frac{67m\pi}{n}}\tan{\frac{\pi}{2}-\frac{109m\pi}{n}}$. Noting $\tan$ is positive in the first quadrant, we have $\tan{\frac{m\pi}{n}}=\sqrt{\tan{\frac{13m\pi}{n}}\tan{\frac{37m\pi}{n}}\tan{\frac{67m\pi}{n}}\tan{\frac{109m\pi}{n}}}$. Now use the fact that $m$ may be less than $71$; small numbers yield intuition! By the scalar triple product, for any vectors $\mathbf{p},$ $\mathbf{q}$ and $\mathbf{r},$
\[\mathbf{p} \cdot (\mathbf{q} \times \mathbf{r}) = \mathbf{q} \cdot (\mathbf{r} \times \mathbf{p}) = \mathbf{r} \cdot (\mathbf{p} \times \mathbf{q}).\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\|^2 \\
&= \boxed{4}.
\end{align*}

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/precalculus/597_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
From the vector triple product identity,
\[\mathbf{a} \times (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{c}.\]Then
\[\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) = (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{a} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{b} = \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 \mathbf{a} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{b}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= \mathbf{a} \cdot (\|\mathbf{b}\|^2 \mathbf{a} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{b}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2.
\end{align*}Since $\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\| = 2,$
\[2 = \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\|^2 = \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2,\]so $\|\mathbf{b}\|^2 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 = (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2 + 2.$

Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2 \\
&= (\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{b}\|^2)^2 - (\|\mathbf{a}\|^2)^2 - (\|\mathbf{b}\|^2)^2 - 2 (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2 + 2.
\end{align*}From $\|\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{b}\|^{2} = \|\mathbf{a}\|^{2}\|\mathbf{b}\|^{2} - (\mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{b})^{2} = 4$, we know that $(\mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{b})^{2} = \|\mathbf{a}\|^{2}\|\mathbf{b}\|^{2} - 4 = \|\mathbf{a}\times\mathbf{b}\|^{2} - 2^{2} = (2)^{2} - 2^{2} = 0$. Thus, $\mathbf{a}\cdot(\mathbf{b}\times (\mathbf{a}\times \mathbf{b})) = (\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{b}\|^2)^2 + 2 =  \boxed{18}$.

We proceed as follows: We use the fact that the second equation can be written as \[\frac{x^3y-xy^3}{2} = \sqrt{3}\quad\Longrightarrow\quad y\cdot\frac{x^2-y^2}{2} = \sqrt{3}.\]Thus, $x^2 = y^2+2\sqrt{3}/y$, so  $$8 = (x^2+y^2)^2 - 8x^2y^2 = \left(\frac{3\sqrt{3}}{y} + y^2\right)^2 - 8\left(y^2+\frac{2\sqrt{3}}{y}\right) = f(y),$$where $f(y)$ is a quadratic in $y^2$ which we can now solve: remark that $f(y)$ is increasing in $[0,\infty)$ and that $f\left(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\right) = (\sqrt{2}+\sqrt{6})^2-8\sqrt{2}\sqrt{6} = 10-4\sqrt{3}$. GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/precalculus/609_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
From the angle addition formula,
\begin{align*}
\sin \left( \theta + \frac{\pi}{4} \right) &= \sin \theta \cos \frac{\pi}{4} + \cos \theta \sin \frac{\pi}{4} \\
&= \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} (\sin \theta + \cos \theta) \\
&= \frac{1}{3}.
\end{align*}Squaring both sides, we get
\[\frac{1}{2} (\sin 2 \theta + 1) = \frac{1}{9},\]so $\sin 2 \theta = \boxed{-\frac{7}{9}}.$

Let the triangle be $ABC,$ where $AB = AC.$  Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$  Let $H$ and $I$ denote the orthocenter and incenter, as usual.  Without loss of generality, we can assume that the inradius of triangle $ABC$ is 1.  As usual, let $a = BC,$ $b = AC,$ and $c = AB.$

[asy]
unitsize(8 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H, I;
real angleA = aCos(1/9);

B = (0,0);
C = (1,0);
A = extension(B, B + dir(90 - angleA/2), C, C + dir(90 + angleA/2));
D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2;
E = (B + reflect(A,C)*(B))/2;
F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2;
H = extension(B,E,C,F);
I = incenter(A,B,C);

draw(A--D,red);
draw(B--E,red);
draw(C--F,red);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(incircle(A,B,C));
draw(B--I);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill);
dot("$I$", I, dir(0));

label("$1$", (H + I)/2, E);
label("$1$", (D + I)/2, E);
label("$\frac{a}{2}$", (B + D)/2, S);
[/asy]

Since $\angle B = \angle C,$ $B = 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2}.$  Then from triangle $BDI,$
\[\tan \angle IBD = \frac{1}{a/2} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Since $\angle IBD = \frac{B}{2} = 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4},$
\[\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a}.\]From triangle $BDH,$
\[\tan \angle HBD = \frac{2}{a/2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From right triangle $ABE,$ $\angle ABE = 90^\circ - A.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\angle HBD &= \angle ABD - \angle ABE \\
&= B - (90^\circ - A) \\
&= A + B - 90^\circ \\
&= A + 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2} - 90^\circ \\
&= \frac{A}{2}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From the equation $\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a},$
\[\frac{\tan 45^\circ - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan 45^\circ \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a},\]or
\[\frac{1 - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Solving, we find
\[\tan \frac{A}{4} = \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}.\]Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \tan \frac{2A}{4} = \frac{2 \cdot \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}}{1 - (\frac{a - 2}{a + 2})^2} = \frac{a^2 - 4}{4a}.\]But $\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a},$ so
\[\frac{a^2 - 4}{4a} = \frac{4}{a}.\]Then $a^2 - 4 = 16,$ so $a^2 = 20.$  It follows that $a = \sqrt{20} = 2 \sqrt{5}.$

Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{16}{8 \sqrt{5}} = \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}.\]Also, $BD = \frac{a}{2} = \sqrt{5},$ so from right triangle $ABD,$
\[AD = \frac{AB}{\tan \frac{A}{2}} = \frac{\sqrt{5}}{2/\sqrt{5}} = \frac{5}{2}.\]By Pythagoras on right triangle $ABD,$
\[AB = \sqrt{5 + \frac{25}{4}} = \frac{3 \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Finally, by the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$
\[\cos A = \frac{\frac{9 \cdot 5}{4} + \frac{9 \cdot 5}{4} - 20}{2 \cdot \frac{9 \cdot 5}{4}} = \boxed{\frac{1}{9}}.\]

Let $ABC$ be an isosceles triangle with $AB = AC$ and let $P$ be the intersection point of the altitudes from $B$ and $C$.  As $AB = AC$, the angle bisector of $\angle A$ is also an altitude of $\triangle ABC$.  Thus, $P$ is also the incenter of $\triangle ABC$.  Let the inradius of $\triangle ABC$ be $r$ and let $M$ be the midpoint of $BC$.  Then $PM=r$, $BM=r \tan \frac{A}{2}$, and from the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle BPM$, $BP=\sqrt{r^2 + r^2 \tan^2 \frac{A}{2}} = r \sqrt{1+\tan^2 \frac{A}{2}} = \frac{r}{\cos \frac{A}{2}}$.  Similarly, $CP=\frac{r}{\cos \frac{A}{2}}$.  Thus, we have the equation $\frac{r}{\cos \frac{A}{2}} + \frac{r}{\cos \frac{A}{2}} = 2 \cdot AB = 2 \cdot AC = 4 \cdot BP \cos \frac{A}{2}$.  Simplifying this expression gives $\cos \frac{A}{2} = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$, so $\cos A = \cos 2\frac{A}{2} = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}}$.

By the Triangle Inequality,
\[\|\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k\| \le  \|\mathbf{v}_1\| + \|\mathbf{v}_2\| + \dots + \|\mathbf{v}_k\| = k.\]Then
\[k \ge \left\|\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{61} > \sqrt{49} = 7,\]so $k \ge 8.$

We can express $\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$ as the sum of 8 unit vectors, as shown below, so the smallest possible value of $k$ is $\boxed{8}.$

[asy]

unitsize(1 cm);

int i;
pair A, B, C;

A = 6*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5));
C = (6,-5);
B = intersectionpoint(arc(A,1,-45,0),arc(C,1,90,180));

for (i = 0; i <= 5; ++i) {
  draw(i*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5))--(i + 1)*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5)),red,Arrow(6));
}
draw(A--B,red,Arrow(6));
draw(B--C,red,Arrow(6));

Since $\|\mathbf{v}_i\| = 1$ for each $i,$ for each $i$ we can write
\[\mathbf{v}_i = \begin{pmatrix} \cos \theta_i \\ \sin \theta_i \end{pmatrix}\]for some angle $\theta_i.$

Then
\[6 = v_{1x} + v_{2x} + \dots + v_{kx} = \cos \theta_1 + \cos \theta_2 + \dots + \cos \theta_k\]and
\[-5 = v_{1y} + v_{2y} + \dots + v_{ky} = \sin \theta_1 + \sin \theta_2 + \dots + \sin \theta_k.\]By Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[(\cos^2 \theta_1 + \cos^2 \theta_2 + \dots + \cos^2 \theta_k)(\sin^2 \theta_1 + \sin^2 \theta_2 + \dots + \sin^2 \theta_k) \ge (\cos \theta_1 \sin \theta_1 + \cos \theta_2 \sin \theta_2 + \dots + \cos \theta_k \sin \theta_k)^2.\]Since $\|\mathbf{v}_i\| = 1,$ $\cos^2 \theta_i + \sin^2 \theta_i = 1$ for each $i.$  Hence, if $S = \cos \theta_1 \sin \theta_1 + \cos \theta_2 \sin \theta_2 + \dots + \cos \theta_k \sin \theta_k,$ then $|S| \le \sqrt{k}.$  Thus,
\[1 \cdot 25 \ge S^2 = (6 + S)^2 \le (6 + \sqrt{k})^2,\]so $-\frac{6 + \sqrt{k}}{31} \le S \le \frac{6 + \sqrt{k}}{31}.$

Hence,
\[\frac{6 - \sqrt{k}}{31} \le -5,\]so $k = \boxed{025}.$

From $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$
\[\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 &= \|\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= 1 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} = \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2}. \quad (*)\]Also from $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$ we can take the dot product with $\mathbf{v},$ to get
\[\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{v} + (\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 1.\]By the scalar triple product, $(\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w},$ so
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = 1 - \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}.\]From equation $(*),$
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= 1 - \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2.
\end{align*}Let $\theta$ be the angle between $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{w}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{u}\|^2 \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \cos^2 \theta \\
&\le \frac{1}{2}.
\end{align*}Equality occurs when $\mathbf{u} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ and $\mathbf{w} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1/2 \\ 1/2 \end{pmatrix},$ so the largest possible value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

From the product-to-sum formula, $\cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ = \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ),$ so
\begin{align*}

&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cos 10^\circ (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} (\cos 10^\circ \cos 120^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right).
\end{align*}Applying the product-to-sum formula again, we get

A $60^\circ$ rotation around the origin in the counter-clockwise direction corresponds to multiplication by $\operatorname{cis} 60^\circ = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i.$

[asy]

dot("$3 \sqrt{3} - 5i$", A, S);
dot("$4 \sqrt{3} + 2i$", (4*sqrt(3),2), NE);
[/asy]

Extracting Answer from: 
Rotating the complex number $3 \sqrt{3} - 5i$ about the origin by 60 degrees counterclockwise gives a complex number that makes an angle of $60^\circ$ with the real axis.  If $z = a + bi$ is this complex number, then $\tan 60^\circ = \frac{b}{a},$ so $b = \sqrt{3} a.$  Furthermore, since $|z| = |3 \sqrt{3} - 5i| = \sqrt{108 + 25} = 13,$ by the Pythagorean Theorem, $a^2 + (\sqrt{3} a)^2 = 169,$ so $a = \frac{13}{2 \sqrt{3}}$ and $b = \frac{13}{2}.$  Thus, the resulting complex number is
\[\frac{13}{2 \sqrt{3}} + \frac{13 \sqrt{3}}{2}i = \boxed{\frac{13}{2} (\cos 60^\circ + i \sin 60^\circ)}.\]Note that this answer is in polar form.

\[(x,y,z) = t (a,b,c) + (0,-2,-5)\]for some scalar $t$ and some direction vector $(a,b,c).$

Also, since $(3,0,-1)$ lies on the line, we have
\begin{align*}
3 &= ta, \\
0 &= tb - 2, \\
-1 &= tc - 5.
\end{align*}Then $a = 3/t,$ $b = 2/t,$ and $c = (-1 - 5t)/t.$  Substituting into $3 = ta,$ we get $t = 3/a.$  Then $b = 2/t = 2a/3$ and $c = (-1 - 5t)/t = (-2a - 5)/3.$

Therefore, the line is
\[\left( x, y, z \right) = \left( 3 - \frac{9}{a}, -\frac{4}{a}, -5 - \frac{2a + 5}{3} \right).\]To find where this line intersects the $xy$-plane, we set $z = 0,$ which gives us
\[a = -\frac{25}{2}.\]Then $(x,y,z) = \boxed{\left( \frac{27}{5}, -\frac{4}{25}, 0 \right)}.$

Note the similarity of the recursion to the angle addition identity
\[\tan (x + y) = \frac{\tan x + \tan y}{1 - \tan x \tan y}.\]We can take advantage of this similarity as follows: Let $f_1 = 3,$ $f_2 = 2,$ and let $f_n = f_{n - 1} + f_{n - 2}$ for all $n \ge 3.$  Let $\theta_n = \frac{f_n \pi}{12}.$  Then $\tan \theta_1 = \tan \frac{\pi}{4} = 1$ and $\tan \theta_2 = \tan \frac{\pi}{6} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}.$  Also,
\begin{align*}
\tan \theta_{n + 2} &= \tan (\theta_{n + 1} + \theta_n) \\
&= \frac{\tan \theta_{n + 1} + \tan \theta_n}{1 - \tan \theta_n \tan \theta_{n + 1}}.
\end{align*}Since the sequences $(a_n)$ and $(\tan \theta_n)$ have the same initial terms, and the same recursion, they coincide.

Since $\tan \theta$ is periodic with period $\pi,$ to compute further terms of $\tan \theta_n,$ it suffices to compute $f_n$ modulo 12:
\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
n & f_n \pmod{12} \\ \hline
1 & 3 \\
2 & 2 \\
3 & 5 \\
4 & 7 \\
5 & 0 \\
6 & 7 \\
7 & 7 \\
8 & 2 \\
9 & 9 \\
10 & 11 \\

12 & 7 \\
13 & 3 \\
14 & 10 \\
15 & 1 \\
16 & 11 \\
17 & 0 \\
18 & 11 \\
19 & 11 \\
20 & 10 \\
21 & 9 \\
22 & 7 \\
23 & 4 \\
24 & 11 \\
25 & 3 \\
26 & 2
\end{array}
\]Since $a_{25} \equiv a_1 \pmod{12}$ and $a_{26} \equiv a_2 \pmod{12},$ the sequence modulo 12 become periodic at this point, with period 12.

Therefore,
\[a_{2009} = \tan \theta_{2009} = \tan \theta_5 = \boxed{0}.\]

First, we wish to find a pattern for the sequence $(a_n).$ To do this, we compute the next few terms: \begin{alignat*}{6}
&a_1 &&= 1 &&\\
&a_2 &&= \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} &&\\
&a_3 &&= \frac{a_1+a_2}{1-a_1a_2} &&= \frac{1+\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}{1-\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}&= \frac{\sqrt{3}+1}{2}\\

Extracting Answer from: 
In general, By DeMoivre's Theorem,
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{cis} n \theta &= (\operatorname{cis} \theta)^n \\
&= (\cos \theta + i \sin \theta)^n \\
&= \cos^n \theta + \binom{n}{1} i \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta - \binom{n}{3} i \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dotsb.
\end{align*}Matching real and imaginary parts, we get
\begin{align*}
\cos n \theta &= \cos^n \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \binom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb, \\
\sin n \theta &= \binom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \binom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
\tan n \theta &= \frac{\sin n \theta}{\cos n \theta} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb}{\cos^n \theta - \dbinom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \tan \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \tan^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \tan^5 \theta - \dotsb}{1 - \dbinom{n}{2} \tan^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \tan^4 \theta - \dotsb}.
\end{align*}Taking $n = 9,$ we get
\[\tan 9 \theta = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Note that for $\theta = 5^\circ,$ $25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $165^\circ,$ $\tan 9 \theta = \tan 45^\circ = 1.$  Thus,
\[1 = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Let $t = \tan \theta,$ so
\[1 = \frac{9t - 84t^3 + 126t^5 - 36t^7 + t^9}{1 - 36t^2 + 126t^4 - 84t^6 + 9t^8}.\]Thus, $\tan 5^\circ,$ $\tan 25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $\tan 165^\circ$ are the roots of
\[t^9 - 9t^8 - 36t^7 + 84t^6 + 126t^5 - 126t^4 - 84t^3 + 36t^2 + 9t - 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, their sum is $\boxed{9}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We can start by using the tangent addition formula repeatedly:
\begin{align*}
\tan 5^\circ + \tan 25^\circ &= \frac{\sin 5^\circ}{\cos 5^\circ} + \frac{\sin 25^\circ}{\cos 25^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 5^\circ \cos 25^\circ + \sin 25^\circ \cos 5^\circ}{\cos 5^\circ \cos 25^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 20^\circ}{\frac{1}{2} \cdot \left( \sin 20^\circ + \sin (-10^\circ) \right)} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{\sin 20^\circ + \sin 170^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{\sin 20^\circ + \sin 10^\circ}
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
\tan 65^\circ + \tan 85^\circ &= \frac{\sin 65^\circ}{\cos 65^\circ} + \frac{\sin 85^\circ}{\cos 85^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 65^\circ \cos 85^\circ + \sin 85^\circ \cos 65^\circ}{\cos 65^\circ \cos 85^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 150^\circ}{\frac{1}{2} \cdot \left( \sin 150^\circ + \sin 80^\circ \right)} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ + \sin 100^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ + \sin 80^\circ}
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
&\tan 5^\circ + \tan 25^\circ + \tan 45^\circ + \dots + \tan 165^\circ \\
&= \left( \tan 5^\circ + \tan 25^\circ \right) + \left( \tan 45^\circ + \tan 65^\circ \right) + \dots + \left( \tan 145^\circ + \tan 165^\circ \right) \\
&= \frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{\sin 20^\circ + \sin 10^\circ} + \frac{2 \sin 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ + \sin 80^\circ} + \dots + \frac{2 \sin 160^\circ}{\sin 20^\circ + \sin 170^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{\sin 20^\circ + \sin 10^\circ} + \frac{2 \sin 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ + \sin 80^\circ} + \dots + \frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{\sin 20^\circ + \sin 170^\circ}.
\end{align*}We can express each sine as a sum of sines with smaller angles:
\begin{align*}
&\frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{\sin 20^\circ + \sin 10^\circ} + \frac{2 \sin 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ + \sin 80^\circ} + \dots + \frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{\sin 20^\circ + \sin 170^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{2 \sin 15^\circ \cos 5^\circ} + \frac{2 \sin 30^\circ}{2 \sin 55^\circ \cos 25^\circ} + \dots + \frac{2 \sin 20^\circ}{2 \sin 95^\circ \cos 85^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin 20^\circ}{\sin 15^\circ \cos 5^\circ} + \frac{\sin 30^\circ}{\sin 55^\circ \cos 25^\circ} + \dots + \frac{\sin 20^\circ}{\sin 95^\circ \cos 85^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 75^\circ}{\cos 5^\circ} + \frac{\cos 60^\circ}{\cos 25^\circ} + \dots + \frac{\cos 75^\circ}{\cos 85^\circ} \\
&= \cos 75^\circ \left( \frac{1}{\cos 5^\circ} + \frac{1}{\cos 85^\circ} \right) + \cos 60^\circ \left( \frac{1}{\cos 25^\circ} + \frac{1}{\cos 65^\circ} \right) + \dots + \cos 75^\circ \left( \frac{1}{\cos 85^\circ} + \frac{1}{\cos 5^\circ} \right) \\
&= 2 \cos 75^\circ \sec 5^\circ + 2 \cos 60^\circ \sec 25^\circ + \dots + 2 \cos 75^\circ \sec 85^\circ \\
&= 2 \cos 75^\circ \cdot \frac{1}{\cos 5^\circ} + 2 \cos 60^\circ \cdot \frac{1}{\cos 25^\circ} + \dots + 2 \cos 75^\circ \cdot \frac{1}{\cos 85^\circ}.
\end{align*}Since $\cos (90^\circ - x) = \sin x$ and $\cos x = \sin (90^\circ - x),$ we can write
\begin{align*}
&2 \cos 75^\circ \cdot \frac{1}{\cos 5^\circ} + 2 \cos 60^\circ \cdot \frac{1}{\cos 25^\circ} + \dots + 2 \cos 75^\circ \cdot \frac{1}{\cos 85^\circ} \\
&= 2 \sin 15^\circ \tan 85^\circ + 2 \sin 30^\circ \tan 65^\circ + \dots + 2 \sin 15^\circ \tan 5^\circ.
\end{align*}We can regroup the terms:
\begin{align*}
&2 \sin 15^\circ \tan 85^\circ + 2 \sin 30^\circ \tan 65^\circ + \dots + 2 \sin 15^\circ \tan 5^\circ \\
&= 2 \sin 15^\circ \left( \tan 85^\circ + \tan 5^\circ \right) + 2 \sin 30^\circ \left( \tan 65^\circ + \tan 25^\circ \right) + \dots + 2 \sin 15^\circ \left( \tan 85^\circ + \tan 5^\circ \right) \\
&= 2 \sin 15^\circ \cdot 2 \tan 45^\circ + 2 \sin 30^\circ \cdot 2 \tan 45^\circ + \dots + 2 \sin 15^\circ \cdot 2 \tan 45^\circ \\
&= 2 \sqrt{2} \left( \sin 15^\circ + \sin 30^\circ + \dots + \sin 75^\circ + \sin 60^\circ \right) \\
&= 2 \sqrt{2} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} \cdot (1 + 2 + \dots + 6) \\
&= \boxed{56}.
\end{align*}We can also prove by induction that $\tan 5^\circ + \tan 25^\circ + \dots + \tan 85^\circ + \tan 65^\circ = 28.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Multiplying both sides by $zw(z + w),$ we get
\[zw = (z + w)^2,\]which simplifies to $w^2 + zw + z^2 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[w = \frac{-1 \pm i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot z,\]so the solutions are $w = z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$ and $w = z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ,$ which means that $P$ is an equilateral triangle.

[asy]
usepackage("amsmath");

unitsize(2 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = dir(20);
B = dir(20 + 120);
C = dir(20 + 240);

draw(Circle((0,0),1));
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0));
draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2));

dot("$z$", A, A);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$", B, B);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ$", C, SW);
[/asy]

The side length of the equilateral triangle is
\[\left| z - \frac{-1 + i \sqrt{3}}{2} z \right| = \left| \frac{3 - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \right| |z| = \sqrt{3} \cdot 2,\]so the area of the equilateral triangle is
\[\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cdot (2 \sqrt{3})^2 = \boxed{3 \sqrt{3}}.\]

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/precalculus/814_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Observe that $w \neq -z.$ Multiplying both sides by $z(z+w)$ gives \[z+w = w(z+w) + z^2 \implies w^2 + (z-1)w + z^2 = 0.\]By the quadratic formula, \[w = \frac{1-z \pm \sqrt{(1-z)^2 - 4z^2}}{2}.\]Since $|z|=2,$ we have $z = 2e^{i \theta}$ for some angle $\theta.$ Then $1-z = -2 \cos \theta - 2 i \sin \theta,$ so \begin{align*}
(1-z)^2 - 4z^2 &= 4 \cos^2 \theta + 8i \sin \theta \cos \theta - 4 \sin^2 \theta - 16 \cos^2 \theta \\
&= -12 \cos^2 \theta + 8i \sin \theta \cos \theta - 4 \sin^2 \theta.
\end{align*}We evaluate the two roots of $w$ separately. First, plugging in the $+$ sign, \begin{align*}
w &= \frac{1-z + \sqrt{(1-z)^2 - 4z^2}}{2} \\
&= -\cos \theta - i \sin \theta + \frac{i}{2} \sqrt{12 \cos^2 \theta - 8i \sin \theta \cos \theta + 4 \sin^2 \theta} \\
&= 2e^{-i \theta} \left(-\cos \theta - i \sin \theta + \frac{i}{2} \sqrt{3} \sqrt{\cos^2 \theta - 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta - \sin^2 \theta}\right) \\
&= 2e^{-i \theta} \left(-\cos \theta - i \sin \theta + \frac{i}{2} \sqrt{3} \left(\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{2} - 2 \theta\right) - i \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2} - 2 \theta\right)\right)\right) \\
&= 2e^{-i \theta} \left(-\cos \theta + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \sin 2 \theta - i \sin \theta - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cos 2 \theta\right).
\end{align*}Next, plugging in the $-$ sign, \begin{align*}
w &= \frac{1-z - \sqrt{(1-z)^2 - 4z^2}}{2} \\
&= -\cos \theta - i \sin \theta - \frac{i}{2} \sqrt{12 \cos^2 \theta - 8i \sin \theta \cos \theta + 4 \sin^2 \theta} \\
&= 2e^{-i \theta} \left(-\cos \theta - i \sin \theta - \frac{i}{2} \sqrt{3} \sqrt{\cos^2 \theta - 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta - \sin^2 \theta}\right) \\
&= 2e^{-i \theta} \left(-\cos \theta + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \sin 2 \theta + i \sin \theta + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cos 2 \theta\right).
\end{align*}Thus, the vertices of the polygon $P$ are \[2e^{i \theta}, 2e^{-i \theta} \left(-\cos \theta + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \sin 2 \theta - i \sin \theta - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cos 2 \theta\right),\]and \[2e^{-i \theta}, 2e^{-i \theta} \left(-\cos \theta + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \sin 2 \theta + i \sin \theta + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cos 2 \theta\right).\]These are six distinct points in the complex plane, so $P$ is a hexagon. We could use the Shoelace theorem to compute its area, but instead we use a trick: from the symmetry in the problem, it is clear that the hexagon consists of 12 congruent triangles whose combined area is half the area of the hexagon, so we can instead find the area of one such triangle and multiply by $6.$ Naive use of the Shoelace theorem would involve lots of nasty algebra, but at this point we can use a more subtle approach:

$\cos 2\theta$ and $\cos \theta$ appear in the expression, so we try writing everything in terms of $\cos \theta.$ Observe that $e^{2i \theta} = (e^{i \theta})^2 = 4,$ so $\cos 2 \theta = 2 \cos^2 \theta - 1.$ Also, recall that $\cos 3\theta = 4 \cos^3 \theta - 3 \cos \theta,$ so \begin{align*}
\cos 3\theta - 3 \cos \theta &= 4 \cos^3 \theta - 4 \cos \theta \\
&= 4 \cos\theta (\cos^2 \theta - 1)\\
&= -4 \cos \theta \sin^2 \theta.
\end{align*}Thus, \begin{align*}
|w|^2 &= 4 - 4 \cos \theta \left(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \sin 2\theta\right) - 4 i \sin \theta \left(-\cos \theta + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \sin 2\theta\right) \\
&\qquad - 4 \cos \theta \left(-\cos \theta + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \sin 2\theta\right) + 4 \left(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cos 2 \theta\right)^2 \\
&= 4 - 4 \sqrt{3} \sin^2 \theta - 4 i \sin \theta \cos \theta + 4 \left(\frac{3}{4} \cos^2 \theta - \frac{1}{4}\right) \\
&= 8 \cos^2 \theta - 4 \sqrt{3} \cos \theta + 1 - 4 i \sin \theta \cos \theta,
\end{align*}and some computation with the Pythagorean identity yields \begin{align*}
|z+w|^2 &= 4 + |w|^2 + 4 \operatorname{Re}(z \overline{w}) \\
&= 13 + 8 \cos^2 \theta - 4 \sqrt{3} \cos \theta - 4 i \sin \theta \cos \theta + 8 \cos \theta (-\cos \theta + \tfrac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \sin 2 \theta) \\
&= 13 + 6 \cos^2 \theta - 4 \sqrt{3} \cos \theta - 4 i \sin \theta \cos \theta - 4 \cos^2 \theta + 4 \sqrt{3} \cos \theta \\
&= 9 + 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta - 2 \cos^2 \theta.
\end{align*}Finally, \begin{align*}
\frac{1}{2i} (z+w)(\overline{z} + \overline{w}) &= \frac{1}{2i} (z \overline{z} + z \overline{w} + w \overline{z} + w \overline{w}) \\
&= \frac{1}{2i} (4 + 2 \operatorname{Re}(z \overline{w}) + |w|^2) \\
&= 4 + 4 \cos^2 \theta - 2 \sqrt{3} \cos \theta,
\end{align*}so by the Law of Cosines, the area of the triangle formed by $0,$ $z,$ and $w$ is \[\frac{1}{2} |z||w| \sin \theta = 2\sin \theta \cos \theta \sqrt{2 - 2 \cos \theta}.\]Therefore, the area enclosed by the hexagon is \[6 \cdot 2 \int_0^\pi \sin \theta \cos \theta \sqrt{2-2\cos \theta} \, d\theta = \boxed{8\sqrt{3} - 6 \pi}.\]Riemann summing $n$ equidistant points on the interval gives a better approximation, but we still must integrate $\sqrt{1-x^2}$ and $\cos x$ (we could avoid one of these integrals by integrating over its derivative instead, but that only saves one line).

In cylindrical coordinates, $z$ simply denotes the $z$-coordinate of a point.  Thus, for a fixed $z$-coordinate $c,$ all the points lie on a plane that is parallel to the $xy$-plane.  The answer is $\boxed{\text{(C)}}.$

[asy]
import three;
import solids;

size(200);
currentprojection = perspective(6,3,2);
currentlight = (1,0,1);
real theta = 120;

draw((-2,0,0)--(2,0,0));
draw((0,-2,0)--(0,2,0));
draw(surface((1,1,0.5)--(1,-1,0.5)--(-1,-1,0.5)--(-1,1,0.5)--cycle),gray(0.99));
draw((0,0,-2)--(0,0,0.2));
draw((0,0,0.5)--(0,0,2));

label("$x$", (2,0,0), SW);
label("$y$", (0,2,0), E);
label("$z$", (0,0,2), N);
label("$z = c$", (-1,1,0.5), E);
[/asy]

The normal vector to the plane will be orthogonal to both
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}\]and
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix}.\]Their cross product is
\[\begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} (b - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(b - 1) \end{pmatrix}.\]By scaling, we can take $\begin{pmatrix} 1/(1 - a) \\ 1/(1 - b) \\ 1/(1 - c) \end{pmatrix}$ as the normal vector.  Since the plane passes through $(0,0,0),$ the equation of the plane is
\[\frac{x}{1 - a} + \frac{y}{1 - b} + \frac{z}{1 - c} = 0.\]Since the plane passes through $(a,1,1),$
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 0.\]Adding 1 to both sides, we get
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + 1 + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 1,\]so
\[\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = \boxed{1}.\]

Subtracting the second column from the first column, we get
\[
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x - 7 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 - 343 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = 7,$ so this is one possible value of $x.$

Subtracting the third column from the first column, we get
\[
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = -2,$ so this is another possible value of $x.$

Expanding the last determinant along the first column, we get
\begin{align*}
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} &= -(x + 2) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} + (x^3 + 8) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 7 & -2 \end{vmatrix} \\
&= -9x^3 + 351x + 630 = 0.
\end{align*}Since we know that $x = 7$ are $x = -2$ are two solutions, we can take out a factor of $x - 7$ and $x + 2$, to get
\[-9(x - 7)(x + 2)(x + 5) = 0.\]Hence, the possible values of $x$ are $\boxed{7, -2, \text{ and } -5}.$

(Alternatively, by Vieta's formulas, the sum of the three roots of $-9x^3 + 351x + 630 = 0$ is 0, so the third root is $-7 - (-2) = -5.$)

Notice that the second and third columns are multiples of each other.  Hence, the determinant is 0 if and only if the first and second (or first and third) columns are linearly dependent.

Thus, we need to find all $x$ such that there exist constants $a,$ $b,$ and $c$ such that
\[
a (1,1,1) + b(x,7,-2) + c(x^3,343,-8) = (0,0,0).
\]That is,
\begin{align*}
a + bx + cx^3 &= 0, \\
a + 7b + 343c &= 0, \\
a - 2b - 8c &= 0.
\end{align*}Subtracting the last two equations, we get $9b + 351c = 0,$ so $b + 39c = 0.$  Then
\begin{align*}
-39a - 39bx - 39cx^3 &= 0, \\
a + 7b + 343c &= 0,
\end{align*}so
\[-39a - 39bx - 39cx^3 + 7a + 49b + 2401c = 0.\]Then
\[32a + 10bx + 10cx^3 = 0.\]Since $b + 39c = 0,$
\[32a - 390c x + 10cx^3 = 0.\]Then
\[32a - 390c x + 10cx^3 + 390c x - 11730c = 0,\]so $32a - 11730c + 10cx^3 = 0.$

Hence,
\begin{align*}
-39a - 39bx - 39cx^3 &= 0, \\
32a - 11730c + 10cx^3 &= 0.
\end{align*}From the first equation, $-39a = bx(39 + 3x^2).$  Since $b + 39c = 0,$
\[b = -39c = \frac{x^2 + 13}{3x^2 + 39} (-39).\]Then
\[ax(39 + 3x^2) = \frac{x^2 + 13}{3x^2 + 39} \cdot 39x^3,\]or
\[39a(39 + 3x^2) = (x^2 + 13) \cdot 13x.\]Since $a$ and $x$ are integers, $x$ must be a divisor of 13.  Checking, we find that the only such divisors are $x = -13,$ $-1,$ 1, and 13.

For $x = -13,$ $-1,$ 1, and 13, we get the solutions
\[-13, -1, 1, \text{ and } 13.\]Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{-13,-1,1,13}.$

We have that
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|}.\]Let $d = \|\mathbf{a}\| = \|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\|.$  Since $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ $\mathbf{c}$ are mutually orthogonal, $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} = 0.$  Hence,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = d^2.\]Also,
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 &= (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \\
&= \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c} \cdot \mathbf{c} + 2(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \\
&= d^2 + d^2 + d^2 \\
&= 3d^2.
\end{align*}Hence, $\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| = d \sqrt{3},$ so
\[\cos \theta = \frac{d^2}{d \cdot d \sqrt{3}} = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}.\]

From the formula for the dot product,
\[(\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot \mathbf{a} = \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| \|\mathbf{a}\| \cos \theta.\]Hence,
\[\frac{(\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot \mathbf{a}}{\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| \|\mathbf{a}\|} = \cos \theta.\]Since $\|\mathbf{a}\| = \|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\|,$ $\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| = \sqrt{3} \|\mathbf{a}\|.$  Also,
[(\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot \mathbf{a}]^2 = (2 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + 2 \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + 2 \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c})^2 = (2 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2)^2 = 4 \|\mathbf{a}\|^4,
so
\[\frac{(\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot \mathbf{a}}{\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| \|\mathbf{a}\|} = \frac{2 \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + 2 \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\sqrt{3} \|\mathbf{a}\|^2} = \frac{2 \|\mathbf{a}\|^2}{\sqrt{3} \|\mathbf{a}\|^2} = \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}.\]Therefore, $\cos \theta = \boxed{\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\angle A,$ $\angle B,$ $\angle C$ form an arithmetic sequence, $2 \angle B = \angle A + \angle C.$  Then
\[3 \angle B = \angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180^\circ,\]which means $\angle B = 60^\circ,$ and $\angle A + \angle C = 120^\circ.$

Let $h$ be the altitude from $B.$

[asy]
unitsize (1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D;

A = (0,0);
B = 5*dir(40);
C = (5,0);
D = (B.x,0);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D);

label("$A$", A, SW);
label("$B$", B, N);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$h$", (B + D)/2, E);
[/asy]

Then
\[h = AB - BC = \frac{h}{\sin A} - \frac{h}{\sin C},\]so $1 = \frac{1}{\sin A} - \frac{1}{\sin C}.$  Hence,
\[\sin C - \sin A = \sin A \sin C.\]We can write this as
\[2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} \cos \frac{A + C}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) - \cos (A + C)}{2}.\]Since $A + C = 120^\circ,$
\[\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) + \frac{1}{2}}{2} = \frac{\cos (C - A) + \frac{1}{2}}{2}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} &= 2 \cos (C - A) + 1 \\
&= 2 \left( 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} \right) + 1 \\
&= 3 - 4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2},
\end{align*}so
\[4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} + 4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 3 = 0.\]This factors as
\[\left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 1 \right) \left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} + 3 \right) = 0.\]Thus, the only possible value of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $x$ be the common difference, so $\angle A = B - x$ and $\angle C = B + x.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\sin \frac{C - A}{2} &= \sin \frac{(B + x) - (B - x)}{2} \\
&= \sin x.
\end{align*}Let $h$ be the length of the altitude from $B$ to $\overline{AC}.$  Then
\[\tan B = \frac{h}{AB - BC},\]so
\[h = (AB - BC) \tan B = (2R \sin A - 2R \sin C) \frac{\sin B}{\cos B} = 2R (\sin A - \sin C) \tan B.\]Also, in $\triangle ABC,$
\begin{align*}
\sin A &= \frac{AC}{2R} = \frac{2R \sin (B - x)}{2R} = \sin B \cos x - \cos B \sin x, \\
\sin C &= \frac{AB}{2R} = \frac{2R \sin (B + x)}{2R} = \sin B \cos x + \cos B \sin x.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[h = 2R (\sin B (\cos x - \cos x) - \sin x (\cos B + \cos B)) = -4R \sin x \cos B,\]so $\sin x = -\frac{h}{4R \cos B}.$

Then
\[\sin x = -\frac{h}{4R \cos B} = -\frac{AB - BC}{4R \cos B}.\]By the Law of Sines,
\[AB - BC = 2R \sin B (1 - \cos A + \cos C),\]so
\[\frac{AB - BC}{2R} = \sin B (1 - \cos A + \cos C) = 2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} (\sin^2 \frac{A}{2} + \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2}).\]Then
\[\sin x = -\frac{h}{4R \cos B} = -\frac{2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} (\sin^2 \frac{A}{2} + \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2})}{\cos B}.\]By the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$
\[\cos B = \frac{\cos A + \cos C}{2},\]so
\[\sin x = -\frac{4 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} (\sin^2 \frac{A}{2} + \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2})}{\cos A + \cos C}.\]Since
\[\cos A + \cos C = 2 \cos \frac{A + C}{2} \cos \frac{A - C}{2} = 2 \cos B \cos \frac{A - C}{2},\]we can write this as
\[\sin x = -\frac{2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} (\sin^2 \frac{A}{2} + \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2})}{\cos B \cos \frac{A - C}{2}}.\]Since $\frac{A + C}{2} = B,$
\[\sin \frac{A}{2} = \cos \frac{B - C}{2}\]and
\[\sin \frac{C}{2} = \cos \frac{B - A}{2}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\sin x &= -\frac{2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} (\sin^2 \frac{A}{2} + \cos \frac{B - C}{2} \cos \frac{B - A}{2})}{\cos B \cos \frac{A - C}{2}} \\
&= -\frac{2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} ((1 - \cos A) + \cos \frac{B - A}{2} \cos \frac{B - C}{2})}{\cos B \cos \frac{A - C}{2}} \\
&= -\frac{2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} ((1 - \cos (B - x)) + \cos \frac{B - A}{2} \cos \frac{B - C}{2})}{\cos B \cos \frac{A - C}{2}} \\
&= -\frac{2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} (\sin^2 \frac{B - x}{2} + \cos \frac{B - A}{2} \cos \frac{B - C}{2})}{\cos B \cos \frac{A - C}{2}} \\
&= -\frac{\sin^2 \frac{B}{2} \cos x + \sin^2 \frac{B}{2} \cos A + \sin \frac{B}{2} \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2}}{\cos \frac{B}{2} \cos \frac{A - C}{2}} \\
&= -\frac{(\sin \frac{B}{2} \cos \frac{B}{2} \cos x + \sin \frac{B}{2} \cos \frac{B}{2} \cos A) + \sin \frac{B}{2} \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2}}{\cos \frac{B}{2} \cos \frac{A - C}{2}} \\
&= -\frac{\sin B \cdot \frac{1}{2} \sin x + \sin B \cdot \frac{1}{2} \sin A + \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2}}{\cos \frac{B}{2} \cos \frac{A - C}{2}} \\
&= -\frac{\sin B \sin \frac{C - A}{2} + \sin B \sin \frac{A}{2} + \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2}}{\cos \frac{B}{2} \cos \frac{A - C}{2}}.
\end{align*}Thus,
\[\sin x = -\tan \frac{B}{2} \tan \frac{A - C}{2} - \tan \frac{B}{2} \tan \frac{A}{2} - \tan \frac{C}{2} \tan \frac{A}{2}.\]Let $\theta = \frac{B}{2}.$  Then $A = B - x$ and $C = B + x.$  Also, $\theta,$ $\theta - \frac{x}{2},$ and $\theta + \frac{x}{2}$ are angles of a triangle, which means
\[\theta + \frac{x}{2} < 90^\circ.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\sin x &= -\tan \theta \tan \frac{x}{2} - \tan \theta \tan \left( \theta - \frac{x}{2} \right) - \tan \left( \theta + \frac{x}{2} \right) \tan \left( \theta - \frac{x}{2} \right) \\
&= -\frac{\sin \theta \sin \frac{x}{2}}{\cos \theta \cos \frac{x}{2}} - \frac{\sin \theta \sin \left( \theta - \frac{x}{2} \right)}{\cos \theta \cos \left( \theta - \frac{x}{2} \right)} - \frac{\sin \left( \theta + \frac{x}{2} \right) \sin \left( \theta - \frac{x}{2} \right)}{\cos \left( \theta + \frac{x}{2} \right) \cos \left( \theta - \frac{x}{2} \right)}.
\end{align*}Since $\sin (\alpha - \beta) - \sin \alpha \cos \beta - \cos \alpha \sin \beta,$ we can write this as
\[\sin x = \frac{\sin^2 \theta - \sin \theta \sin \frac{x}{2} \cos \theta - \sin^2 \left( \theta - \frac{x}{2} \right)}{\sin \frac{x}{2} \cos \frac{x}{2}} - \frac{\sin x}{\cos^2 \left( \theta + \frac{x}{2} \right)}.\]Hence,
\[\sin x \left( 1 + \frac{1}{\cos^2 \left( \theta + \frac{x}{2} \right)} \right) = \frac{\sin^2 \theta - \sin \theta \sin \frac{x}{2} \cos \theta - \sin^2 \left( \theta - \frac{x}{2} \right)}{\sin \frac{x}{2} \cos \frac{x}{2}}.\]We see that $x$ cannot be zero, so we can divide both sides by $\sin x (1 + \sec^2 (\theta + \frac{x}{2})).$  This gives us
\[\tan x = \frac{\sin \theta \cos \theta - \sin \theta \cos (\theta - x) - \sin (\theta - x) \cos \theta}{\cos \theta \cos (\theta - x) \cos (\theta + \frac{x}{2})}.\]Since $\sin \theta \cos (\theta - x) + \cos \theta \sin (\theta - x) = 2 \sin \theta \cos \theta \cos \frac{x}{2}$ and $\sin (\theta - x) = \sin (\theta + x),$ we can simplify this to
\[\tan x = \frac{4 \sin \theta \cos \theta \sin^2 \frac{x}{2}}{\cos \theta \cos (\theta - x) \cos (\theta + \frac{x}{2})}.\]Let $t = \tan \theta,$ so $\cos \theta = \frac{1}{\sqrt{t^2 + 1}}$ and $\sin \theta = \frac{t}{\sqrt{t^2 + 1}}.$  Then
\[\cos (\theta - x) = \frac{1 - tx}{\sqrt{t^2 + 1}},\]and
\begin{align*}
\cos (\theta + \frac{x}{2}) &= \cos \theta \cos \frac{x}{2} - \sin \theta \sin \frac{x}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{\sqrt{t^2 + 1}} \sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos x}{2}} - \frac{t}{\sqrt{t^2 + 1}} \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos x}{2}}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\begin{align*}
\tan x &= \frac{4 \sin \theta \cos \theta \sin^2 \frac{x}{2}}{\cos \theta \cos (\theta - x) \cos (\theta + \frac{x}{2})} \\
&= \frac{4t(t - x)(1 + t^2) \sin^2 \frac{x}{2}}{(1 - tx)(1 - (t - x)^2)(1 + \cos x + \sqrt{t^2 + 1} \sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos x}{2}} - t \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos x}{2}})}.
\end{align*}Let $y = \frac{tx}{t - x},$ so $x = \frac{ty}{1 + y}$ and
\[\tan x = \frac{t^2 y}{1 + y}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\frac{4t(t - x)(1 + t^2) \sin^2 \frac{x}{2}}{(1 - tx)(1 - (t - x)^2)(1 + \cos x + \sqrt{t^2 + 1} \sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos x}{2}} - t \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos x}{2}})} &= \frac{4t \cdot \frac{(1 + y)^2 - ty}{1 + y} (1 + t^2) \sin^2 \frac{ty}{2(1 + y)}}{(1 - t^2) \left( 1 - \frac{ty}{t - x} \right) \left(1 - \frac{(t - x)^2}{t^2}\right) \cdot 2 \cos^2 \frac{x}{2}} \\
&= \frac{(1 + y)(1 + t)(1 - t)(1 + ty)^2 \sin^2 \frac{ty}{2(1 + y)}}{(1 - ty)(t - x)^2 (1 - t^2)(1 + \cos x + \sqrt{t^2 + 1} \sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos x}{2}} - t \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos x}{2}})}.
\end{align*}To reduce this expression further, let $w = \frac{ty}{2(1+y)}.$  Then $y = \frac{2w}{t-w},$ and
\[\sin^2 \frac{ty}{2(1+y)} = \frac{\sin^2 w}{1+\frac{2w}{t-w} \sin^2 w}.\]Also,
\[\sqrt{t^2+1} \sqrt{\frac{1+\cos x}{2}} - t \sqrt{\frac{1-\cos x}{2}} = \frac{2 \sin^2 \theta}{\sqrt{t^2+1}+\cos \theta} - t \frac{2 \sin^2 \theta}{\sqrt{t^2+1}+\cos \theta} = 2 \sin^2 \theta \cdot \frac{(t-\sqrt{t^2+1})(\sqrt{t^2+1}+\cos \theta)}{\sqrt{t^2+1}(\sqrt{t^2+1}+\cos \theta)} = 2 \sin^2 \theta (t-\sqrt{t^2+1}).\]Thus,
\[\tan x = \frac{\frac{1}{4} (1 + y)(1 + t)(1 - t)(1 + ty)^2 \sin^2 w/t}{(1 - ty)(t - x)^2 (1 - t^2)(1 + \cos x + 2 \sin^2 \theta (t - \sqrt{t^2+1}))}.\]Since
\[\cos A = 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{A}{2},\]we can write
\[\cos B = 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2}\]and
\[\cos C = 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{C}{2}\]in terms of $\sin \frac{A}{2},$ $\sin \frac{B}{2},$ and $\sin \frac{C}{2}.$

Thus, we can write an expression for $\sin x$ solely in terms of $\sin \frac{A}{2},$ $\sin \frac{B}{2},$ and $\sin \frac{C}{2}.$  To summarize:
\begin{align*}
\sin \frac{C - A}{2} &= \sin x \\
&= \frac{(1 + y)(1 + t)(1 - t)(1 + ty)^2 \sin^2 w/t}{\frac{1}{4} (1 + y)(1 + t)(1 - t)(1 + ty)^2 \sin^2 w/t + (1 - ty)(t - x)^2 (1 - t^2)(1 + \cos x + 2 \sin^2 \theta (t - \sqrt{t^2+1}))}.\end{align*}where $x$ and $y$ satisfy $x = \frac{ty}{1+y}$ and $y = \frac{2w}{t-w},$ and where
\begin{align*}
\sin \theta &= \frac{t}{\sqrt{t^2 + 1}}, \\
\cos \theta &= \frac{1}{\sqrt{t^2 + 1}}, \\
\sin \frac{A}{2} &= \cos \frac{B - C}{2}, \\
\sin \frac{B}{2} &= \cos \frac{C - A}{2}, \\
\sin \frac{C}{2} &= \cos \frac{A - B}{2}, \\
w &= \frac{ty}{2(1+y)}, \\
\cos A &= 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{A}{2}, \\
\cos B &= 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{B}{2}, \\
\cos C &= 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{C}{2}.
\end{align*}There are four solutions, obtained from the four solutions to $t^2 + 1 = 2t \cos B$:
\[\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}, \frac{\sqrt{5}}{10}, \frac{\sqrt{5}}{6}, \frac{5 + 2 \sqrt{5}}{10}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Note that $\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} = \|\mathbf{v}\| \|\mathbf{w}\| \cos \theta,$ where $\theta$ is the angle between $\mathbf{v}$ and $\mathbf{w}.$  Therefore,
\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} = 12 \cos \theta.\]By Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[(3^2 + 4^2)(\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w})^2 \ge (3 \cdot 4 \cos \theta)^2.\]Hence,
\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} \ge \frac{9}{5},\]so the minimum value is $\boxed{\frac{9}{5}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix}$ is the normal vector, its projection is the zero vector.  Thus,
\[\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5} \begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{5}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{5}{14} \end{pmatrix} \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then $\frac{13}{14} a - \frac{1}{7} b + \frac{3}{14} = 0,$ $-\frac{1}{7} a + \frac{5}{7} b + \frac{3}{7} c = 0,$ and $\frac{3}{14} a + \frac{3}{7} b + \frac{5}{14} = 0.$  These reduce to
\begin{align*}
13a - 2b + 3c &= 0, \\
-a + 5b + 3c &= 0, \\
3a + 6b + 5c &= 0.
\end{align*}Subtracting the first two equations, we get $14a - 7b = 0,$ so $b = 2a.$  Then
\[-a + 10a + 3c = 0,\]so $c = -3a.$  Hence,
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a \\ 2a \\ -3a \end{pmatrix} = a \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix},\]so the vector we seek is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}}.$

GPT File: datasets/persona_confidence_copy/persona_confidence_copy/precalculus/993_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Let $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix}$ be a vector in $\mathbb{R}^3.$  Let $P$ be the projection of $\mathbf{v}$ onto the plane.  Then the vector $\mathbf{w} = \mathbf{v} - \mathbf{p}$ is orthogonal to the plane, where $\mathbf{p}$ is the projection of $\mathbf{v}$ onto the plane.

[asy]
import three;

size(160);
currentprojection = perspective(6,3,2);

triple I = (1,0,0), J = (0,1,0), K = (0,0,1);
triple O = (0,-0.5,0), V = (1.5,1,0.7), P = (V.x,V.y,0), Q = (V.x,0,0);

draw(surface((1,-1,0)--(1,1,0)--(-1,1,0)--(-1,-1,0)--cycle),paleyellow,nolight);
draw((1.2,0,0)--(-1.2,0,0),black+1bp,EndArrow(5));
draw((0,-1.2,0)--(0,1.2,0),black+1bp,EndArrow(5));
draw((0,0,-0.5)--(0,0,1.2),black+1bp,EndArrow(5));
draw(O--V,red,Arrow3(6));
draw(P--V,dashed,Arrow3(6));
draw(O--P,dashed,Arrow3(6));
draw(V--Q,dashed,Arrow3(6));
draw(rightanglemark(O,P,V,2));

label("$\mathbf{v}$", V, N, fontsize(10));
label("$\mathbf{p}$", P, S, fontsize(10));
label("$\mathbf{w}$", V + (0,0.1,-0.1), E, fontsize(10));
label("$\mathbf{p}$", Q, SE, fontsize(10));
[/asy]

Thus,
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} x - \frac{1}{7} y + \frac{3}{14} z \\ -\frac{1}{7} x + \frac{5}{7} y + \frac{3}{7} z \\ \frac{3}{14} x + \frac{3}{7} y + \frac{5}{14} z \end{pmatrix}\]must be parallel to the plane.  This gives us
\[\begin{pmatrix} x - \frac{13}{14} x + \frac{1}{7} y - \frac{3}{14} z \\ \frac{1}{7} x - \frac{5}{7} y - \frac{3}{7} z \\ \frac{3}{14} x + \frac{3}{7} y + \frac{5}{14} z - z \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} a - \frac{1}{7} b + \frac{3}{14} c \\ -\frac{1}{7} a + \frac{5}{7} b + \frac{3}{7} c \\ \frac{3}{14} a + \frac{3}{7} b + \frac{5}{14} c \end{pmatrix}\]for some constants $a,$ $b,$ and $c.$  Thus,
\begin{align*}
\frac{13}{14} x - \frac{1}{7} y + \frac{3}{14} z &= \frac{13}{14} a - \frac{1}{7} b + \frac{3}{14} c, \\
-\frac{1}{7} x + \frac{5}{7} y + \frac{3}{7} z &= -\frac{1}{7} a + \frac{5}{7} b + \frac{3}{7} c, \\
\frac{3}{14} x + \frac{3}{7} y + \frac{5}{14} z &= \frac{3}{14} a + \frac{3}{7} b + \frac{5}{14} c.
\end{align*}Then $7x - 2y + 3z = 7a - 2b + 3c,$ $2x - 10y + 6z = 2a - 10b + 6c,$ and $3x + 6y + 5z = 3a + 6b + 5c.$  Solving, we find $2x + y + z = 2a + b + c.$

Since $\mathbf{v}$ is arbitrary, this means the plane $P$ is given by the equation $2x + y + z = 0.$  Hence, the normal vector of the plane is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}.$
